- 6 Basic Rules For Pairing Food With Wine (Video): Kind: captions Language: en Hi, I'm Rachel May and today we're going to talk about wine pairings. Now, there's certain elements of wine. There's sugar, acid, fruit, tannins, and alcohol. And there's also flavor components of food. There's fat, acid, salt, sweet, bitter, and texture. So, when we think about pairing, we need to think about how these flavor elements are going to work together. Let's talk about some of the basic rules of wine pairing. Rule number one, hors d'oeuvres need a dry rosé. The reason is a rosé combines the light crispness of a white with the fruitiness of a red. And if you have a table of hors d'oeuvres, there's many different flavors and textures that you need to accommodate. Rule number two, acid needs acid. So, any food that has a high acidic level, something you'd want to squeeze a lemon onto, that's a great pairing with a light citrusy acidic white wine. If you have a dish like chicken piccata, pasta with tomato sauce, or a roasted fish with citrus, which will pair with a bright citrusy Pinot Grigio or Sauvignon Blanc. It'll be like a zap of fresh lemon in your dish. Rule number three, tannins need fat. If you have food with a higher fat content, say a marbled ribeye steak, or a braised duck, or grilled sausages, you really need a wine with a textural element that will balance it out. If you pair with a bold red wine like a Syrah or a Cabernet, the tannins in that red wine really balance out the fat element. Rule number four, heat needs sweet. If you're serving a dish that has a little kick to it or a little bit of spice, you're going to need a lighter slightly sweet wine to pair with it. A good example would be a Riesling or a white Zinfandel. Rule number five, salty needs bubbles. Now, it can be a little hard to pair salty or fried foods with a wine, but similar to a beer, the carbonation of the sparkling wine adds a whole different texture and brings a whole new flavor to it. Rule number six, earthy needs earthy. Now, if you have a earthier food like mushrooms, lentils, or gamey meats, they're going to be great paired with an earthy wine. A good choice would be a Syrah or a Pinot Noir. You really want to think about your dominant flavors. Is it sweet? Is it salty? Is it citrusy? These are the elements you want to consider when pairing your wine with your entrée. Hopefully, this gives you a better idea for how to pair your wines with your food. These tips are brought to you by Shoptopia, just what you want, right where you are.

- How To Choose Wine For Dinner: Kind: captions Language: en Hi, I'm Paul Quaglini, wine expert with ABC Fine Wine & Spirits. And I'm Nathan Dale, also wine expert with ABC Fine Wine & Spirits. And today we're going to talk about food and wine pairing for your dinner tonight. [Music] When it comes to pairing food and wine, there are basically a couple of schools of thought. One is the traditional where there are strict rules and you must pair this wine with this food or else your dinner is going to be ruined. Well, it's terrible and you ruined a fun thing. And then there's the other train of thought where people think, ah, just drink whatever wine you want with whatever food you want. Uh, no rules, just a total anarchy system. Rules are, there ain't no rules. I don't think that's particularly helpful. I think somewhere in the middle where you don't follow a strict set of rules, but rather a set of guidelines is much more helpful. Pairing wine and food is about textures and flavors, but ultimately if you are cooking fish, you think lighter bodied wines. But if that fish has a cream sauce, you're no longer pairing the fish, you're pairing the dominant flavor in the dish. Food and wine pairing is very, very broad. There are scientific reasons, there are emotional reasons, some things work better than others. If you like it and it works, then that's fine, that rule doesn't apply. A few guidelines I like to follow are basically foods with a lot of acid like wines with a lot of acid. Grilling chicken on a hot summer day, I love to pair rosé because it's got acidity and it's not sweet. Misnomer, not all rosé is sweet. And it pairs beautifully with a nice chicken seared with a little bit of barbecue sauce. Mm. Foods that have a lot of fat love wines with a lot of tannin. Getting really nerdy, proteins bond to tannins. Cabernet is king, that's why a big fat steak works so well. Wines that have a bit of earthiness to them like a French Pinot Noir pair perfectly with foods that have a bit of earthiness to them, some wild mushrooms for example. One of my very favorite pairings is pork and Chianti. Sangiovese works so beautifully with pork. Salty foods like bubbles. Believe it or not, potato chips and sparkling wine are a perfect pairing. So if you really want to know what my favorite pairing is, hit that like, subscribe, and notification bell. So as a lover of all things Tuscany, I love making red sauces using a Chianti Classico. I certainly cook with it. I'll put a glass or so into the sauce itself, but I obviously keep the rest of the bottle to drink with the meal because a wine that you're cooking with is going to be the wine you want to drink with. If you keep in mind a few of these simple guidelines, it'll make wine and food pairing much more simple. At the very essence of pairing food and wine, it's based on the dominant flavor. Whatever the biggest flavor in the food is is how you're going to pair your wine. So you may not pair the wine to the steak if it's bathed in rosemary. Wherever the largest flavor is is what you want to pair your wine to. As wine educators, really one of our most important things is to take the snobbery and pretense out of wine and wine and food pairing in general. So again, follow a few simple guidelines and then just have fun with it. Experiment. Let your palate be your own best judge and just enjoy. So now that you've gotten comfortable pairing food and wine, come visit us at one of our ABC Fine Wine & Spirits locations. We have tons of Chianti Classico and a plethora of other wines for whatever dish you're deciding to make. And check out some of our other videos.

- The Sixty Vines Hack: How to Host a Group Dinner for 14 on a Budget: Kind: captions Language: en You just crushed the ELSAT. Or maybe someone is graduating and you need to celebrate with a dozen friends. Instantly, what should be a fun night turns into a stressful math equation. Booking a dinner for 14 people usually ends up being a logistical and financial nightmare. Most hosts trying to plan a group dinner immediately fall into a rigid binary trap. You look around the city and realize you only have two distinct paths. Option A is the casual route. A local dive or a sports bar keeps the individual cost low, but you sacrifice the upscale celebratory atmosphere that a major life event demands. Option B is traditional fine dining. You get the white tablecloths and the perfect vibe, but those massive restaurant markups immediately shatter your budget ceiling of 20 to $40 a person. This decision tree maps out what you're actually searching for, the missing middle. To hit that $30 sweet spot, you have to find a venue that balances a premium aesthetic with strict cost controls. 60 Vines in Houston's Rice Village fills this gap by treating group dining as a logistical problem to be solved through its specific business model. To see if it actually works for a large group, we evaluate their location logistics, their unique beverage system, and how their menu is structured. A venue that mathematically protects the host from surprise overages removes the primary stressor of group hosting. When the system handles the budget, the host is free to actually participate in the celebration instead of quietly panicking over the final check. The primary pivot happens behind the bar. Instead of storing individual glass bottles, 60 Vines runs a 60 tap system, pouring wine directly from high volume kegs. This eliminates a massive amount of physical waste. A single keg replaces 26 glass bottles. Over its refillable lifetime, one of these kegs diverts over 1500 bottles, corks, labels, and shipping foils away from landfills. Looking at this data comparison table, you can see how a zerowaste supply chain drastically reduces the restaurant's overhead. Without the packaging, shipping weight, and spoilage risks of traditional glass, the structural cost of maintaining a wine seller drops significantly. Those operational savings lower the entry price for premium wine, keeping many glasses under $10. The menu offers four variable tap pores, a 2.5 oz tasting, a 5 oz glass, an 8 oz glass, and a 750ml carff. For a group of 14, this creates incredible flexibility. Guests can explore high-end varietals using the small tasting pores without forcing the host to commit $60 or more to a single bottle. There is a trade-off. By moving to a tap system, diners have to sacrifice the classic tableside cork popping ritual usually associated with fine dining. Trading that performative cork pop for absolute budget control is a massive net positive for an anxious host. You get the premium liquid without the premium risk. But the drinks are only half the equation. Walking into 60 Vines without a strict ordering strategy for the food menu will absolutely break your budget. This quadrant chart maps their menu by cost and sharability. The danger zone is right here in the top right quadrant. If your guests start ordering individualized premium entre like the wood grilled bevette steak or the filetmenan, your $40 limit is destroyed. The host's primary defense mechanism is to take control of the ordering early. You have to steer the table toward the high return communal items. The safe zone sits in the top left quadrant. This means ordering rounds of artisan pizzas like the fig and pushcido or the margarita alongside large shakurerie boards and placing them directly in the center of the table. This mimics a tapa style experience. It forces your guests to interact, pass plates, and try a wider variety of flavors. This shared plate strategy naturally curbs over orderering and eliminates the waste of halfeaten heavy entre. Executed correctly, it effectively caps the final food bill at 20 to $30 per head. Controlling the center of the table gives your guests the illusion of a lavish, boundless feast while rigidly protecting your wallet from individualized spending spikes. Next, we have to look at the physical location. Rice Village is a dense commercial district infamous for high friction parking and crowded sidewalks. Getting a dozen people to arrive at once is a headache. The restaurant's interior layout actually solves for the seating side of this equation. They have dedicated communal tables designed specifically to seat groups of 8 to 16 people comfortably, so you aren't crammed into a corner. For the cars, the venue has a built-in parking perk. Guests receive exactly 2 hours of free parking in the main garage or rooftop lot. However, that 2-hour limit is a strict trap. Group meals naturally run long, especially when you factor in toasts and staggered arrivals. The required mitigation here is simple but crucial. As the host, you must instruct your guests to download the Park mobile app beforehand. This lets them seamlessly add time from the table, avoiding parking fines. Addressing parking and seating friction before arrival ensures a flawless Nilestone dinner. Removing these hurdles allows the group to transition to the table without losing momentum. So, does 60 Vines actually operate as a group dining hack for a 20 to $40 budget? Let's look at this split screen scorecard mapping the restaurant's business model against your specific needs. On the left, the ideal match is the logistical host. If you are planning a graduation or milestone for 8 to 15 people, and you demand financial predictability, you should book this. It's also a perfect match for the eco-conscious diner and the valued-driven wine explorer who wants to test a variety of 2.5 ounce pores without breaking the bank. On the right, we have the anti-match. You should pass if you have a group of traditional fine dining purists expecting a hushed, intimate room with formal tableside bottle service. You should also avoid this venue if your party absolutely insists on ordering separate heavy entre rather than participating in a shared menu. But if you can adapt to the tap system, the space effectively emulates its target aesthetic. You get the open, airy feel of California wine country without requiring a flight to the West Coast. The keg and pizza model inherently insulates you against the massive unpredictable markups that usually ruin the experience of hosting a large group. For a highly specific 20 to $40 per person target, leaning into the tap pores and a shared plate strategy makes this one of the most mechanically sound choices in the city.

- What Should You Do When A Wine Is Corked At A Dinner Party? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en What should you do when a wine is cked at a dinner party? Imagine pouring a glass of wine only to notice a strange smell or taste that doesn't seem right. You might wonder if the wine is spoiled or just off. Sometimes a wine can be cked, which is a common flaw caused by a chemical compound called TCA. This compound contaminates the cork and transfers to the wine, giving it a musty, moldy, or wet cardboard aroma. You might also notice a flat or muted flavor profile that doesn't match the wine's usual character. Recognizing these signs is the first step. Take a gentle sniff and swirl the wine in your glass. If you detect a damp basement smell, mold, or wet dog scent, it's likely cked. It's important to confirm the flaw before reacting. If you're unsure, compare it with a wine you know is fresh or ask someone nearby for their opinion. Once you're confident the wine is cked, the best move is to discreetly tell the host or the person who brought the bottle. They might want to replace it, especially if they have another bottle on hand. If you're the host, apologize and offer an alternative. If you have other bottles with screw caps or synthetic corks, those are unlikely to be affected. Remember, cork taint only affects wines sealed with natural corks. If no replacement is available, it's best to stop drinking the cked wine. The flaw is chemical and cannot be fixed by irerration or decanting. Pour it out quietly to avoid disappointing other guests or causing confusion. From a wine appreciation point of view, experiencing a cked wine can be a good learning moment. Keep a small taste to recognize the signs in the future. Some people are more sensitive to TCA than others, so sharing your observations can help confirm the flaw. It's also a chance to practice your sensory skills and become better at identifying wine flaws. In summary, when you encounter a ced wine at a dinner party, look for the musty or moldy aromomas and flat flavors. Inform the host or replace the bottle if possible. Avoid drinking the flawed wine since it won't improve. Use this experience to sharpen your ability to detect cork taint and enjoy wine tasting even more. [Music]

- Fête Des Vignerons  - Switzerland's Biggest Wine Festival | DW Euromaxx: Kind: captions Language: en A toast to wine itself at the wine festival in Vevey on Lake Geneva. This lavish procession is one of the highlights of Switzerland's biggest wine festival. It's only held once every 20 to 25 years as a tribute to the work in the vineyards - right down to fighting the pests that attack the grapes. The procession is led by the Hundred Swiss, one-time bodyguards to the French king. Now, they protect the wine-growers as guardsman Patrick Frasseren explains. "Why every 20 to 25 years? The festival is to be held only once a generation, as decreed by the Vevey Fraternity of Winegrowers. It's a celebration of the winegrowers who work the vineyards all year. And we crown the best." A frequent sight in the region are these tractors, used to carry loads through the vineyards. Sitting on the loadbed is wine-grower Jean Duboux. At the last wine festival, he was just ten years old. "It's like a great big party. We meet lots of people from our own troupe and performers from other troupes, as well. And here in Vevey, there are lots of wine cellars along the lakeshore that we run and where we can go have a glass together." The festival reaches its climax with the crowning of the wine-grower king in the arena. In 20-19, it's 56-year-old Jean-Daniel Berthet. "The wine-growers who work with the vines are monitored by the wine-growers' fraternity. They developed a point system for it that's been used for a few years now. The wine-growers' fraternity visits the different vineyards and assigns grades. And whoever gets the best results is crowned king." The festival has been well documented over its more than three centuries long history. Every festival is more extravagent than the last. Now, fans not only come from all over Switzerland, but from around the world. Months of work have gone into building this ultra-modern stadium especially for the 20-19 festival. It can seat 20-thousand spectators - enough for the coronation festivities. A play is staged in the arena - another tribute to wine-growing, of course. It's a highlight that's held several times during the 25-day festival. Playing cards symbolize the whims of nature, which brings good and bad vintages. The grapes on the renowned Terraces des Lavaux grow right above Lake Geneva, where the surrounding mountains protect them from the wind and cold weather. Wine-grower Guillaume Potterat and his family have their vineyard here, as well. "What makes the vineyards in the Lavaux special are our three suns: the sun in the sky that shines on the terraces; the reflection of the sun in the lake and then the stone walls that store heat and release it onto the slopes. That's what's special about the slopes here." The Potterat family has been growing wine for generations. Father, son and uncle meet up in the morning to taste their light, dry white wine made from the Chasselas grape. They still own the wine press used by their great, great grandfather Jean-Francois Potterat. "They started making wine in the Lavaux back in the 11th century. The Cistercian monks established vineyards all over Europe. They planted the first vines on the steep slopes, because they owned that land, and because there wasn't much else you could use it for." Back in Vevey, standard-bearers from many of the Swiss cantons march along the lakeshore promenade. Guillaume Potterat is handing the tradition on to his family's next generation. "The last time was in 1999, and I took part myself. Now I'm very happy to be showing my daugter the festival. It's a major event in Switzerland, and as a wine-grower it makes me very proud." This year, the festival runs till August the 11th in Vevey on Lake Geneva, with many attactions to come. Anyone who misses it will have to wait at least twenty years for the next one.

- The Sommelier's Secret  Decoding the Investment Value of Rare Wine: Kind: captions Language: en Take a moment and pour yourself a glass of wine. What you hold in your hand is a beverage meant for enjoyment, for a quiet evening, a celebration, or a dinner party. Yet for a select few, that same liquid in a different bottle is not a drink, but a high-performing tangible asset. A centuries old commodity now traded in a digital marketplace. We are talking about the secret world of fine wine investment where a single corkked bottle can be worth more than your car, your annual salary, or even your house. This is not about the latest Cabernet you picked up at the store. This is about a highly specialized asset class where scarcity, reputation, and immaculate history converge to create astonishing value. Today, we're decoding the sumeier's secret. What transforms simple fermented grape juice into a multi-million dollar investment? and how this liquid asset class is defying conventional finance. The engine of value, rarity, ageability, and provenence. What separates a $1,000 bottle from a $20 bottle? The answer is simple in theory, complex in execution. Longevity, scarcity, and providence. First, a wine must have the structure to age. Investment grade wines, the creme de la creme of the global market, are built to improve for decades. This means high levels of acidity, balanced tannins, and concentrated fruit designed to soften and harmonize over time. As the wine ages, it enhances its complexity, flavor, and quality, becoming more desirable. Crucially, as the quality improves, the supply diminishes.3. Every time a bottle is consumed, the remaining stock becomes instantly rarer. This creates the classic supply and demand dynamic that drives prices higher. The more coveted and scarcer the bottle, the higher the value it commands on the secondary market. Four, the vast majority of the world's wine is not made for this. Less than 1% of global production qualifies as true investment grade wine. Five. Finally, and perhaps most importantly, there is provenence. Provenence is the documented history of the bottle. Who owned it, where it was stored, and how it was transferred. Six. In a market plagued by sophisticated fraud like the notorious case of Rudy Kernan, Providence is the assurance of authenticity. A bottle with an impeccable paper trail verified by a reputable auction house or storage facility can be worth dramatically more than an identical bottle with an undocumented or questionable past. Seven, this focus on verifiable history ensures that you are buying the legend, not the lie. The Somier's secrets decoding the investment mechanics. Investing in wine works differently than stocks or real estate. It operates largely through two core mechanisms. The entreur system and the secondary market tracked by sophisticated indices. The entreur or wine futures or market is dominated by Bordeaux. Investors purchase wine while it is still in the barrel, often 18 to 24 months before it is bottled and physically released. They receive a certificate of ownership, paying a discounted price in anticipation of the wine's quality and its future scarcity. This system allows merchants to finance the vintage and allows investors to secure bottles from highly sought-after producers before they hit the general market, maximizing their potential profit margin. Once released, bottles enter the secondary market where they are traded between collectors, brokers, and investment platforms. 10. This market is benchmarked by the LiveX London International Ventner Exchange indices such as the Livx 100 or the LiveX 1000.11. These indices track the price performance of the most traded wines, providing transparency and data that was once reserved only for the elite few. 12. This data confirms that fine wine is not merely a hobby. It is a measurable asset class that over the long term has historically offered returns competitive with traditional equities all while acting as a hedge against inflation and broader economic downturns. The trading volume and price movements on the secondary market are what truly determine the investment value of any rare wine. The blue chips regions and producers that command value. The vast majority of investment grade wine originates from a handful of prestigious old world regions. Though the market is steadily diversifying. 13. These are the blue chip names that anchor any serious wine portfolio. Bordeaux remains the cornerstone. 14. The five first growths. Premier Crew Class A Chateau Lafit Rothchild, Chateau Lur, Chateau Muton Rothchild, Chateau Margo, and Chateau Hobo Brion are the equivalent of the Dow Jones Industrial Average. Their track record for quality and longevity in exceptional vintages like 1982, 2000, 2005, and 2009 is unmatched, making them essential reliable investments. Burgundy represents the ultimate expression of scarcity. 15. Due to the fractured ownership of tiny vineyard plots, climate producers like Domain Dea Roman Conti, DRC, Domain Laroy, and Armon Rouso produce minuscule quantities of GR crew wines. When these bottles hit the market, particularly from celebrated vintages, their rarity ensures prices soar, making Burgundy the sector most prone to astronomical returns. 16. The high demand and microscopic supply mean Burgundy prices are often the most volatile, but also the most potentially rewarding. Beyond France, key players include the super tuscans from Italy, such as Sasakaya and Medo, which blend traditional Italian wine- making with French varietals to create internationally acclaimed ageworthy wines. 17 prestige couet champagne led by brands like Dome Perinon and Louis Ruter's Cristal has also solidified its position offering stable returns and a high degree of liquidity. Finally, a small but powerful contingent of Cult Nappa Cabernet, Screaming Eagle, Harland Estate and Scarecrow have established a robust secondary market driven by intense US collector demand and exceptionally low production. These global names are the foundation upon which a fine wine portfolio is built. The crucial role of condition protecting your liquid gold. For a wine to maintain its value, it must be stored in near-perfect conditions. 18. This is where the concept of storing in bond becomes paramount. Storing wine in bond means placing it in a professionally managed temperature and humidity controlled warehouse typically in the UK that has been approved by customs and excise 19. This professional storage protects the wine from the four great enemies heat, light, vibration and temperature fluctuation.20 20 optimal conditions are a steady temperature of around 55° F, 13° C, and 70 to 80% humidity. 21 improper storage, even for a short time, can damage the wine, leading to poor fill levels or cork failure, drastically reducing its value 22. Beyond preservation, in bond, storage provides critical financial benefits. 23 wine stored this way remains exempt from local value added tax and duty.24. When the investor sells the wine, it can be transferred still in bond to another investor or merchant without ever incurring these taxes, making the asset much more attractive and liquid on the international market. 25. For an investor, the paper trail generated by professional bonded storage is the ultimate proof of providence. 26. No serious buyer will touch a wine that has been stored in a private home seller or worse, an unverified location, regardless of the label. Investing in wine means investing in its vault. The risks and rewards of the fine print. As with any asset class, wine investment carries unique risks alongside its substantial rewards. 27. On the risk side, wine is fundamentally a collectible, not a company, and it does not pay dividends. 28. It also carries high associated costs. Professional storage fees, insurance, and brokerage commissions for buying and selling can erode returns if not accounted for. 29. Furthermore, while fine wine is less volatile than many equities, it is an illquid asset. It may take weeks or months to find the right buyer, especially during market downturns. 30. The biggest non-financial risk remains fraud and poor provenence, underscoring the necessity of purchasing only through reputable brokers or auction houses. 31. The rewards, however, are compelling. Wine offers a crucial diversification benefit, often performing independently of the broader stock market, thereby hedging risk. 32. It is a tangible asset that can be used to pass wealth through generations. And unlike stocks, a case of first growth bordeaux from an exceptional vintage is a finite diminishing resource, a true store of wealth. 33. For many, the reward is also emotional. Owning a piece of history, an exquisite work of art that can be opened and enjoyed at its peak maturity. Conclusion: The new portfolio of passion. The investment world has changed. The sumeier secret that the world's finest wines are a stable, high-erforming asset is now out. The modern rare wine market is a fascinating blend of old world tradition and new world technology, leveraging digital indices and bonded warehouses to trade a perishable luxury good. It is a sector where passion meets profit and where the decisions of a few hundred producers in France, Italy, and California determine the value of a global commodity. To invest in rare wine is to invest in patience, history, and the beautiful certainty of finite supply. If this deep dive into the paradoxical world of wine investment captured your imagination, be sure to give this video a like, share this video with a fellow collector or investor, and most importantly, subscribe to our channel for more fascinating looks into the intersection of finance and luxury.

Especially in a recession when luxury spending dries up, you might have to sell at a big loss. There's no instant sell button. >> Okay, so the value is fuzzy. Selling is slow. What else? The costs don't stop when you buy it, I assume. >> Not even close. Transaction costs are notoriously high. Auctions charge big buyer and seller premiums. That adds a hefty chunk. Then you've got ongoing costs. Specialized insurance. Forget your standard homeowners policy for a million dollar painting. Secure climate controlled storage, >> which is essential, right? >> Absolutely critical for art, wine, lots of things. Plus maybe transport costs. All these eat away at your potential returns big time. >> You mentioned storage. So, I can't just like stick a rare comic or that fancy Bordeaux in the attic. >> Oh god, no. Not if you want it to hold its value or in the case of wine, not turn into vinegar. Proper storage is key and it's expensive. Art needs specific temperature, humidity. Wine needs cool, dark, humid conditions. Cars need climate control, maintenance. They're not passive assets. They need constant costly care >> and the risk of fakes. Fraud >> that's always looming, especially in high-V value markets like art or vintage cars, counterfeits, messed up provenence. It's a real risk. You need serious expertise for due diligence. Finding out your masterpiece is a fake. That's your whole investment gone. Poof. >> And finally, I guess taxes and legal stuff add another layer of pain. >> Definitely. Collectibles often get taxed differently. In the US, the capital gains tax rate on collectibles is higher, maxing out at 28% versus the standard long-term rate. plus state taxes, then inheritance, gift taxes. If you buy internationally, import duties, VAT, different legal rules. It gets incredibly complicated. You often need specialist adviserss. >> Wow. Okay. Suming that up. Huge cost, need expert knowledge, hard to sell, value is subjective, high fees, expensive upkeep, risk of fakes, complex taxes. Yeah, that list makes it pretty clear why this was mostly a playground for the mega rich. >> It really paints the picture of the traditional scene. It wasn't necessarily designed to be exclusive, but the nature of the assets just demanded huge resources. >> Okay, but here's where it gets really interesting where the sources show a massive shift directly challenging that for rich Americans only idea, >> right? You're talking about the rise of fractional ownership platforms. This is probably the biggest thing to hit the collectible markets accessibility in well generations. >> Explain that. How does it work? If I know about buying shares in Google, how is this different for say a painting? >> The basic idea is pretty neat actually. Simple concept, complex execution. A company, a platform goes out and buys a really high value collectible. Let's say a million-doll painting or maybe a super rare comic. >> Okay? >> They handle all those barriers we just talked about. They have the experts to find and authenticate it. They put up the cash to buy it. They arrange the professional storage, insurance, all that. >> So, they own the whole expensive thing. Then what? Then they basically securitize it. Fancy word, but it just means they divide the ownership, the value of that one big item into lots and lots of small digital shares or units. >> Yeah. Like slicing up a pizza >> kind of. Yeah. And then they offer those small slices, those shares to the public. So lots of different people can buy a tiny piece of that big expensive asset. You don't own the physical thing, but you own a fraction of its value. >> And the appeal for someone like me or you listening is obvious. >> It's way cheaper to get in. Exactly. That's the main draw. Instead of needing millions for the painting, you might buy one share from them. So, well, the sources mentioned minimums as low as $10, $20, maybe $100 on some platform. >> Seriously, 10 bucks? >> Yeah. For certain items. Others dealing with really high-end stuff might have higher minimums, maybe $250, $1,500, or even $10,000. But compare that to the price of the whole asset. It cracks the door wide open to markets that were completely locked before. And it lets you spread your bets too, right? Not put everything on one painting. >> Yes. Another huge advantage with lower minimums. You don't have to put, say, your entire $10 savings into one piece of art, which is super risky. You can diversify. Buy a small share of a Banky, a piece of a vintage Ferrari, maybe a bit of a rare baseball card across different platforms. But here's where the source made a really crucial distinction impacting logistics and especially taxes. Are you buying it to drink yourself or purely to sell later for profit? >> Ah, okay. They mention storing theirs in a professional offsite climate controlled warehouse essential to keep it perfect. >> And if you're just speculating, trying to flip it, >> then the wine is often held in bond. That means it stays in a special government supervised bonded warehouse, often in places like the UK or Europe. The big advantage, you never take personal delivery. This makes it much easier to sell later, especially internationally. And crucially, it lets you defer certain taxes like VAT in Europe until the wine leaves the bond, which might only be when the next buyer takes it out years later. >> Right. Taxes. The sources went deep on wine tax, and honestly, it sounds like a minefield, especially in the US. Can you boil down the absolute key tax points for a wine investor? >> You're right. It's super complex and the source material was detailed. The number one thing for US investors, the IRS classifies wine as a collectible. >> And that means >> it means higher taxes on your profits. Long-term capital gains selling wine held over a year are taxed at a maximum federal rate of 28%. That's significantly higher than the usual 20% max for stocks or real estate. Short-term gains taxed as ordinary income potentially up to 37%. >> Ouch. And states take a bite, too. >> Oh, yeah. State capital gains taxes pile on top. California's top rate is over 13%. Plus, you might pay sales tax when you buy it, federal excise tax, import duties if it comes from abroad. It adds up. >> So, US taxes aren't great for wine investors. How's it look across the pond in Europe where so much investment wine is traded? That's where it gets really interesting and potentially better for some investors. Many European countries treat wine differently, often as a cattle, basically movable personal property, which can mean lower taxes. >> Like where any examples? >> Sure. The UK has a helpful capital gains tax exemption. If a bottle or case sells for under £6,000, the profit is usually tax-free. Above that, rates are lower than the US collectible rate, like 10 20%. France generally exempts private sales by hobbyists, but taxes professional trading heavily, though gains become taxfree after holding for 22 years. Germany hold it over a year and gains are usually CGT exempt. Italy also tends to exempt private sales and has lower inheritance tax rates. >> And the VAT thing you mentioned with bonded warehouses, >> that's huge. European VAT on wine is typically 1925%. If you keep it stored in bond, you don't pay that VAT or import duties until it's physically removed for drinking. If you sell it while it's still in bond, transferring ownership within the warehouse system, you might never pay the VAT to yourself. That's a massive cost saving for investors. >> So bonded storage is as much about tax strategy as preservation. >> Absolutely. For serious international wine investors, it's standard operating procedure, essential for both. >> What about passing down a collection? Inheritance tax sounds tricky, too. It is a valuable wine collection counts as part of your taxable estate. In the US, the federal estate tax kicks in above a very high threshold like $12.92 million in 2024, but rates go up to 40%. States can have their own inheritance taxes, too. Europe varies. Italy has lower rates. Germany has decent allowances for family. >> With all this complexity, are there ways wine investors try to manage the tax hit? >> Yes, the sources mention a few tactics. Using bonded warehouses is key, especially in Europe. leveraging that UK 6K chatt exemption, holding wine longer in places like France or Germany for tax breaks. In the US, using annual gifting allowances, that 17K per person gift tax exclusion can help reduce your estate size over time. Donating to museums might work in some places like France. But the big takeaway, you need experts, tax pros, estate planners who really get the specifics of wine and international rules. Don't DIY this. - The Entire Process of Making Wine: Kind: captions Language: en wine is known as the nectar of the Gods it's a fascinating drink that elevates the spirit and intoxicates the senses but how much of the process involves stomping the grapes with feet and how is wine actually made let's take a look at that and a whole lot more in this video wine making can be traced back to Egypt as far as 5,000 BC archaeologists have found cave wall paintings not only showing the use of wine but wine jars as well at some point in the past one makers started employing foot trading or stomping to extract juice from The Grapes while this may sound unusual the process has its benefits first the pressure applied with feet was relatively gentle and helped avoid the release of bitter tannin or ar stringent compounds secondly due to the low temperature of the human body the process did not release excessive heat maintaining the desired conditions for fermentation but we're getting ahead of ourselves let's start at the beginning the wine making process begins with the careful selection of grapes the choice of grape variety depends on the type of wine desired as different grapes contribute unique flavors Aromas and characteristics factors such as climate soil composition and Vineyard location also play crucial roles in grape selection once the grapes have reached optimal ripeness they are harvested the timing of the Harvest is critical as it significantly influences the flavor profile of the wine you see if the grapes are under rpe they may have low sugar levels low alcohol and high acidity on the contrary if the grapes are overripe they have the opposite characteristics a perfect balance therefore must be struck when harvesting grapes to ensure that the wine is the best possible for this purpose vineyardist inspect sample of clusters of wine grapes with a refractometer to determine if the grapes are ready to be picked the refractometer is a small handheld device the size of a miniature telescope that allows the the vineyardist to accurately check the amount of sugar in the grapes if the grapes are ready for picking a mechanical Harvester usually a suction picker gathers and funnels The Grapes into a field Hopper or mobile storage container these mechanical Harvesters or in some cases robots are now used in most medium to large Vineyards thereby eliminating the need for handpicking which was the norm in the past first used in California vineyards in 1968 mechanical Harvesters significantly decreased the time it takes to gather grapes the Harvesters have also allowed grapes to be gathered at night when they are cool fresh and ripe after harvesting the grapes are transported to the winery for processing the first step is crushing where the grapes Skins are broken to release the juice as mentioned before traditionally this was done by stomping on the grapes and some wineries still employ this process as a way of preserving the craftsmanship of the past however in modern times wineries usually employ the services of a crushed stemmer machine some crushed stemmer machines are hydraulic While others are driven by air pressure the grapes are crushed and the stems are removed leaving liquid must that flows either into a stainless steel fermentation tank or a wooden vat for white wine all the grape Skins are separated from the must or Wine Juice by filters or centrifuges before they undergo fermentation for red wine the whole crushed grape including the skin goes into the fermentation tank or vat this is because during fermentation red wines undergo mation a process where the grape skins remain in contact with the fermenting juice this extracts color tannins and additional flavors from the Skins during the fermentation process wild yeast are fed into the tank or vat to turn the sugar in the must into alcohol some wine makers may also used cultured yeast for their wines as far as the type of yeast goes it can VAR wine makers are continuously experimenting with different kinds of yeast to improve the quality of their wine in addition cane or Beach sugar may be added to increase the alcoholic content adding sugar is called chaptalization usually chaptalization is done because the grapes have not received enough Sun prior to harvesting the wine maker will use a handheld hydrometer to measure the sugar content in the tank or vat the wine must ferment in the tank or vat for approximately 7 to 14 days depending on the type of wine being produced after crushing and fermentation wine needs to be stored filtered and properly aged many wineries still store wine in damp Subterranean wine cellers to keep the wine cool but larger wineries now store wine above ground in epoxy lined and stainless steel tanks the tanks are temperature controlled by water that circulates inside the lining of the tank shell wine can also be stored in Oak barrels or concrete vets Oak aging imparts additional flavors and Aromas to the wine influencing its complexity and structure the duration of Aging varies depending on the wine style and wine maker preferences to remove any remaining sediment or particles the wine is clarified through fining and filteration fining agents such as Bentonite or egg whites are added to bind with undesirable particles allowing them to settle and be easily removed filter ation further refines the Wine's Clarity in some cases wine makers May Blend Wines from different batches or grape varieties to achieve a desired flavor profile this is common in regions where multiple grape varieties are grown providing wine makers with a palet of flavors to work with once the wine maker is satisfied with the Wine's characteristics it is time for bottling the wine is carefully transferred from aging vessels to bottles and in some cases a small amount of sulfur dioxide is added to prevent oxidation and microbial spoilage after bottling the wine is sealed with a cork or alternative closure such as a screw cap most medium to large sized wineries now use automated bottling machines and most moderately priced and expensive wine bottles have corks made of a special Oak the corks are covered with a peel off aluminum foil or plastic seal cheaper wines have an aluminum screw off cap or plastic stopper the choice of closure can impact the Wine's aging potential and susceptibility to oxidation finally labels are applied providing essential information about the wine including its origin vintage and alcohol content if you're curious about the word vintage it refers to the year in which the grapes used to make the wine were harvested it is a way of indicating the specific growing season and weather conditions in a particular gear which can significantly impact the characteristics and quality of the wine wine enthusiasts often pay attention to the Vintage because variations in weather conditions such as temperature rainfall and sunlight during the growing season can influence the ripening of the grapes and consequently the flavor Aroma and overall profile of the wine certain years may be considered better or worse for grape growing and this can be reflected in the perceived quality of the wines produced in a given vintage in some wine producing regions certain years may be declared as exceptional leading to a higher demand and potentially higher prices for wines from that vintage it's important to note that not all wines are vintage dated some are non-vintage Blends that combine wines from different years to achieve a consistent flavor profile while some wines are ready for consumption shortly after bottling others benefit from further Aging in the bottle this aging period allows the wine to evolve and develop more complex flavors and Aromas the optimal aging time varies depending on the wine type and individual preferences all facets of wine production must be carefully controlled to create a quality wine such variables as the speed with which the harvested grapes are crushed the temperature and timing during both fermentation and aging the percent of sugar and acid in the harvested grapes and the amount of sulfur dioxide added during fermentation all have a tremendous impact on the quality of the finished wine that was all about the Journey of a grape from The Vineyard to a wine bottle if you're curious about how sugar is made check out our video on the production of sugar

- WSET Wine Service Series - Serving Red Wine: Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Serving red wine. When the host is ready, take their order. Just for the wine, please. In order to avoid any mistakes, repeat the order back to the host. Select the red wine glasses and place on the table in the correct position. In the cellar, select the wine. Double check it is the correct bottle. Then carefully carry the bottle to the table. Present the bottle making sure the label is visible. Repeat the order back to the host, including the name, vintage, and producer. Wait for the host to approve. When opening the wine, it is best to use a side table or geridon. First, using a waiter's friend, remove the capsule and wipe the top of the bottle with a service cloth. Place the point of the screw in the center of the cork. Twist and push gently until the screw reaches the end of the cork and then gently lever the cork out of the bottle. Next, wipe around the inside of the neck. Now, take the bottle to the table. Hold the bottle, ensuring the label is visible. Pour a sample for the host and wait for their approval. Moving clockwise, pour the wine for the other guests. When pouring, make sure the bottle doesn't touch the glass. Fill the glass to its widest point. Catch any drips with a service cloth. Fill the host's glass last. Finally, place the bottle on a coaster. At some points during the meal, the guests will require topups. Use your judgment to assess when to do so. It is important not to top up too frequently as this can irritate customers. When you do top up, refill the guests glass before refilling the hosts. Then place the bottle back on the coaster.
